## Domain Knowledge (LLM-generated)
**Domain**: chemical reaction kinetics
**Analysis**: This task models the rate of change of concentration dA/dt as a function of concentration A and time t, which is the fundamental problem in chemical kinetics — determining the rate law. The rate typically depends on powers of concentration, with possible time-dependent forcing or complex mechanistic forms.

**Common formula structures in this domain**:
- Zero-order kinetics: dA_dt = c0 (constant rate, independent of concentration)
- First-order kinetics: dA_dt = c0 * A (rate proportional to concentration, radioactive decay, simple decomposition)
- Second-order kinetics: dA_dt = c0 * A**2 (bimolecular reaction of same species)
- General n-th order kinetics: dA_dt = c0 * A**c1 (fractional or integer order rate law)
- Reversible first-order: dA_dt = c0 * A + c1 (first-order with back reaction or constant source/sink)
- Mixed order / Langmuir-Hinshelwood: dA_dt = c0 * A / (c1 + A) (surface-catalyzed or enzyme-like kinetics)
- Michaelis-Menten form: dA_dt = c0 * A / (c1 + A)**2 (substrate inhibition variant)
- Second-order with equilibrium: dA_dt = c0 * A**2 + c1 * A + c2 (quadratic rate with reversibility)
- Autocatalytic reaction: dA_dt = c0 * A * (c1 - A) (logistic-type autocatalysis)
- Time-dependent forcing with first-order: dA_dt = c0 * A * exp(c1 * t) (photochemical or temperature-ramped reaction)
- Parallel reaction pathways: dA_dt = c0 * A + c1 * A**2 (competing first and second order)
- Power-law with time modulation: dA_dt = c0 * A**c1 * t**c2 (aging or diffusion-limited kinetics)
- Inhibition kinetics: dA_dt = c0 * A / (c1 + A**2) (substrate inhibition)
- Consecutive reaction intermediate: dA_dt = c0 * exp(c1 * t) + c2 * exp(c3 * t) (sum of exponentials for intermediate species)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Pure concentration dependence (no explicit t) → autonomous ODE rate law, suggesting elementary or composite reaction mechanism
- Rational function of A (numerator/denominator polynomials in A) → enzyme kinetics or surface catalysis mechanism with adsorption/desorption steps
- Product form A*(c-A) → autocatalytic or logistic growth where both reactant and product concentrations matter
- Negative sign in rate law → consumption/decay of reactant; positive → production or autocatalysis
- Fractional power of A (A**0.5, A**1.5) → complex mechanism with chain reactions or diffusion-limited steps
- Explicit time dependence beyond A → non-isothermal conditions, photochemical forcing, or hidden variable elimination
- Sum of terms with different powers of A → parallel competing reaction pathways
- Denominator polynomial in A → quasi-steady-state approximation applied to intermediate species in multi-step mechanism
- Square root of A → dissociative equilibrium or radical chain initiation step

Key observations about the GT:

**Candidate 1:** A³c0 + A²c1 + Ac2 + A^c3·c4·cos(Ac5)

Wait, sin with a phase shift is fine since sin(x+π/2) = cos(x), but we need sin(kA) specifically. Actually if c3=0, we get c4·cos(Ac5). We can write cos(Ac5) = sin(Ac5 + π/2). So this becomes A²c1 + c4·sin(Ac5 + π/2). The GT is A²c0 + c·sin(kA). Since sin(kA + π/2) = cos(kA) ≠ sin(kA) in general, we can't match exactly... Wait, but we could also try c3=1: Ac4·cos(Ac5). That's A·cos(kA), which is a different basis function. Hmm.

Actually wait - can I set c4=0 to kill the cos term entirely? Then I'd have A²c1, missing the sin term. No match unless... Let me reconsider. The candidate doesn't have a plain sin term. The trig term is always multiplied by A^c3. If c3=0, we get c4·cos(Ac5), which equals c4·sin(Ac5 + π/2). This IS a sin function with a phase shift. But GT has sin(0.7617A) with no phase shift. Can sin(kA + φ) = sin(kA)? Only if φ = 2nπ. But here φ = π/2 fixed from the cos→sin conversion. So this gives cos, not sin. 

Hmm, but actually the candidate uses cos(Ac5). If I want to match sin(0.7617A), I need cos(Ac5) = sin(0.7617A)/c4. Since cos(x) = sin(x + π/2), I'd need Ac5 + π/2 = 0.7617A, meaning c5 = 0.7617 and π/2 = 0... contradiction. So no exact match with c3=0.

What about other c3 values? We need A^c3·cos(Ac5) to equal (const)·sin(kA). For this, c3 must be 0 (otherwise we have A^c3 multiplying), and then cos ≠ sin.

- Set c1=0, c2=0, c4=0: A²c0 + c5·cos(Ac6). cos ≠ sin.
- Could combine Ac2·cos(Ac3) with other terms? This gives A·cos, not sin.
